        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625"><meta property="og:title" content="Rational Design of Three-Dimensional Graphene Encapsulated with Hollow FeP@Carbon Nanocomposite as Outstanding Anode Material for Lithium Ion and Sodium Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/acsnano.7b06625.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Transition metal phosphides have been extensively investigated owing to their high theoretical capacities and relatively low intercalation potentials vs Li/Li+, but their practical applications have been hindered by low electrical conductivity and dramatic volume variation during cycling. In this work, an interesting strategy for the rational design of graphene (GR) encapsulated with a hollow FeP@carbon nanocomposite (H-FeP@C@GR) via a combination of a hydrothermal route, a carbon-coating process, phosphidation treatment, and carbothermic reaction is reported. The hollow FeP (H-FeP) nanospheres shelled with thin carbon layers are wonderfully incorporated into the GR matrix, interconnecting to form a three-dimensional (3D) hierarchical architecture. Such a design offers distinct advantages for FeP-based anode materials for both lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium ion batteries (SIBs). For example, the 3D omnibearing conductive networks from the GR skeleton and outer coating carbon can provide an open freeway for electron/ion transport, promoting the electrode reaction kinetics. In addition, the wrapping of an H-FeP nanosphere in a thin carbon layer enables the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the carbon layer surface instead of on the individual H-FeP surface, preventing the continual re-forming of the SEI. When used as anode materials for LIBs and SIBs, H-FeP@C@GR exhibited excellent electrochemistry performances."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/acsnano.7b06625.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6ee100a91775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ee100a91775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'dae0662f-ed9d-46d4-b2f0-23c9fffdf4df'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90f6ee100a91775c-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ee100a91775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90f6ee100a91775c-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "ancac3";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Rational Design of Three-Dimensional Graphene Encapsulated with Hollow FeP@Carbon Nanocomposite as Outstanding Anode Material for Lithium Ion and Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b06625", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Rational Design of Three-Dimensional Graphene Encapsulated with Hollow FeP@Carbon Nanocomposite as Outstanding Anode Material for Lithium Ion and Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b06625", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Rational Design of Three-Dimensional Graphene Encapsulated with Hollow FeP@Carbon Nanocomposite as Outstanding Anode Material for Lithium Ion and Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b06625", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Rational Design of Three-Dimensional Graphene Encapsulated with Hollow FeP@Carbon Nanocomposite as Outstanding Anode Material for Lithium Ion and Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b06625", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Rational Design of Three-Dimensional Graphene Encapsulated with Hollow FeP@Carbon Nanocomposite as Outstanding Anode Material for Lithium Ion and Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b06625", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Rational Design of Three-Dimensional Graphene Encapsulated with Hollow FeP@Carbon Nanocomposite as Outstanding Anode Material for Lithium Ion and Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b06625", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Rational Design of Three-Dimensional Graphene Encapsulated with Hollow FeP@Carbon Nanocomposite as Outstanding Anode Material for Lithium Ion and Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b06625", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Rational Design of Three-Dimensional Graphene Encapsulated with Hollow FeP@Carbon Nanocomposite as Outstanding Anode Material for Lithium Ion and Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b06625", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Rational Design of Three-Dimensional Graphene Encapsulated with Hollow FeP@Carbon Nanocomposite as Outstanding Anode Material for Lithium Ion and Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b06625", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D11602%26pageCount%3D15%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DXiujuan%2BWang%252C%2BKai%2BChen%252C%2BGang%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D11%26contentID%3Dacsnano.7b06625%26title%3DRational%2BDesign%2Bof%2BThree-Dimensional%2BGraphene%2BEncapsulated%2Bwith%2BHollow%2BFeP%2540Carbon%2BNanocomposite%2Bas%2BOutstanding%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BLithium%2BIon%2Band%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D15%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D11616%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2017" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0012.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0012.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Transition metal phosphides have been extensively investigated owing to their high theoretical capacities and relatively low intercalation potentials <i>vs</i> Li/Li<sup>+</sup>, but their practical applications have been hindered by low electrical conductivity and dramatic volume variation during cycling. In this work, an interesting strategy for the rational design of graphene (GR) encapsulated with a hollow FeP@carbon nanocomposite (H-FeP@C@GR) <i>via</i> a combination of a hydrothermal route, a carbon-coating process, phosphidation treatment, and carbothermic reaction is reported. The hollow FeP (H-FeP) nanospheres shelled with thin carbon layers are wonderfully incorporated into the GR matrix, interconnecting to form a three-dimensional (3D) hierarchical architecture. Such a design offers distinct advantages for FeP-based anode materials for both lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium ion batteries (SIBs). For example, the 3D omnibearing conductive networks from the GR skeleton and outer coating carbon can provide an open freeway for electron/ion transport, promoting the electrode reaction kinetics. In addition, the wrapping of an H-FeP nanosphere in a thin carbon layer enables the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the carbon layer surface instead of on the individual H-FeP surface, preventing the continual re-forming of the SEI. When used as anode materials for LIBs and SIBs, H-FeP@C@GR exhibited excellent electrochemistry performances.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D11602%26pageCount%3D15%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DXiujuan%2BWang%252C%2BKai%2BChen%252C%2BGang%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D11%26contentID%3Dacsnano.7b06625%26title%3DRational%2BDesign%2Bof%2BThree-Dimensional%2BGraphene%2BEncapsulated%2Bwith%2BHollow%2BFeP%2540Carbon%2BNanocomposite%2Bas%2BOutstanding%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BLithium%2BIon%2Band%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D15%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D11616%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Ions" class="article__tags__link">Ions</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292200&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Layers" class="article__tags__link">Layers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292533&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanocomposites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanocomposites" class="article__tags__link">Nanocomposites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292609&amp;ref=fulltext" hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Fep&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_FeP">FeP</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Coating&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon%20coating">carbon coating</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Graphene&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_graphene">graphene</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_batteries">batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=DFT+Calculations&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_DFT%20calculations">DFT calculations</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div id="ac_i1" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h2 class="article-section__title section__title" id="_i1"> </h2><div class="NLM_p">Rechargeable lithium ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely used in portable electronics, electric vehicles, and stationary energy storage because of their advantages of high energy, long lifespan, and environment benignity over other alternatives. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1-5)</a> However, considering the limited and unevenly distributed availability of Li deposits on the earth, LIBs have not been able to meet tremendous demand for large-scale storage systems, leading to the reality that its price will become an important issue for future LIB development. To address these concerns, rechargeable sodium ion batteries (SIBs), as an alternative to LIBs, have received increasing attention in recent years, owning to the wide availability of sodium resources ubiquitous around the world, potential low cost, and similar chemistry to LIBs. Nevertheless, compared to LIBs, the more sluggish kinetics occurs in insertion/extraction of SIBs due to the larger sodium ionic radius, resulting in poor electrochemical performance, which becomes the specific hindrance for future SIB advancement. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6, 7)</a> Therefore, it is imperative for scientists to advance the battery storage technology in both LIBs and SIBs so as to support certain applications. One practical approach in this direction is to design and synthesize appropriate electrode materials that possess the capability of playing a dual storage role for both lithium and sodium.</div><div class="NLM_p">Among various negative electrode materials, transition metal phosphides (TMPs) have been extensively investigated owing to their high theoretical capacities and relatively low intercalation potentials <i>vs</i> Li/Li<sup>+</sup>. So far, many transition metal phosphides, such as Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> Cu<sub>3</sub>P, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> Ni<sub>2</sub>P, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> MoP, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> CoP, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> VP, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> and ZnP<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> have displayed good electrochemical performances in lithium and/or sodium storage. Recently, iron phosphide (FeP), as one kind of transition metal phosphide, having a theoretical capacity of ∼926 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, has been proven to be one of the most promising candidates as anode materials for LIBs and SIBs enlisting a reversible two-step insertion/conversion process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15-17)</a> In a detailed reaction route, FeP, with an orthorhombic structure, reacts fully with lithium or sodium through the conversion reaction FeP + 3Li/Na → intermediate tetragonal LiFeP/NaFeP phase → Li<sub>3</sub>P/Na<sub>3</sub>P + Fe<sup>0</sup> in its first discharge cycle, resulting in a nanocomposite expressed by nanosized Fe<sup>0</sup> particles embedded in a Li<sub>3</sub>P or Na<sub>3</sub>P matrix (illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a). Moreover, within the FeP unit cell, the Fe–P distances are between 2.186 and 2.447 Å, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> larger than the lithium ion diameter 1.08 Å and sodium ion diameter 2.04 Å, which favors the immigration of Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the FeP crystal structure, ameliorating the ionic conductivity and reducing the volume change to some degree during ion insertion and desertion. Despite FeP having several obvious advantages over conventional oxide and sulfide materials, the most intractable obstacles are still hindering the future development of anode materials, including (1) inevitable volume variation during charge and discharge, which not only leads to serious electrode material pulverization and loss of the electrical contact between active materials and the current collector but also makes it difficult to coat a stable protection layer to maintain the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), and eventually results in serious capacity fading during long-term cycling; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 19 to 23">(19-23)</a> (2) low electronic conductivity, hampering the fast transfer of electrons inside the active materials and confining their electrochemical reactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24, 25)</a> To cope with these problems, the most commonly used approach is to design a nanoscale FeP coated with a carbon layer. The protective carbon shell acts as a physical buffering layer for the large volume change (cushion effect), improves the electronic conductivity through intimate contact between carbon and FeP, and prevents FeP pulverization during the Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> insertion–extraction process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 26 to 30">(26-30)</a> For instance, recently, Han <i>et</i><i>al</i>. reported that a nanoplate FeP shelled with a carbon layer had a high reversible lithium storage capacity of up to 720 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a capacity retention rate of 96% after 100 cycles at a current density of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> He and his co-workers also synthesized an amorphous and mesoporous FeP modified with a dual-carbon phase (carbon coating and CNTs) used as an anode material for SIBs, which demonstrated a utilization rate of 78% for the active material and a reversible capacity of 415 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Even though the rate capability and cycle life of the nanostructures have been markedly enhanced by coating amorphous carbon on the surface of FeP as an outer conductive layer, the electrochemical performance improved by the single carbon coating is still far from meeting the requirements for large-scale energy storage. For example, a single introduced carbon matrix into nanostructured FeP cannot maintain the structural stability for a very long cycling, and the individual outer carbon coating cannot fully ensure a fast ion/electron transfer across the interfaces. Hence, an upgraded strategy combining a design of hollow nanostructure FeP and a double carbon network modification consisting of an amorphous carbon layer and 2D graphene (GR) is proposed by ourselves to solve the aforementioned challenges. Compared to the previously reported work, the hollow void space of FeP can accommodate a huge volume change during cycling, delaying capacity fading. More importantly, GR, a two-dimensional (2D) carbon nanostructure, has been recently considered as a promising material for energy storage due to its superior electrical conductivity, excellent mechanical flexibility, large specific surface area, and good thermal and structural flexibility. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 32 to 34">(32-34)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Crystal structure evolution of FeP during the conversion reaction with lithium or sodium. (b) Schematic illustration of the synthesis process for the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we attempt to design and fabricate a GR encapsulated with a hollow FeP@carbon nanocomposite (H-FeP@C@GR) <i>via</i> a combination of hydrothermal reaction, carbon coating process, phosphidation treatment, and carbothermic reaction (illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). The hollow FeP (H-FeP) nanospheres shelled with thin carbon layers are well incorporated into the GR matrix, interconnecting to form a three-dimensional (3D) hierarchical architecture. The crucial roles of GR on upgrading a simple FeP@carbon nanocomposite to a complex 3D network structure nanocomposite are as follows: (1) The 3D omnibearing conductive networks from the GR skeleton and outer carbon coating can provide rapid diffusion channels for electron/ion transport, promoting the electrode reaction kinetics. (2) The GR, as a cage-like framework around the H-FeP@C nanospheres, is of importance to prevent the agglomeration of active materials and cracking of the electrode. (3) The large volume change of H-FeP@C during the Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> insertion–extraction process can be buffered through accommodation by the elastic GR along with available internal voids, ensuring the structural integrity of the electrode. As a result, when used as anode materials for LIBs and SIBs, the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite exhibited high reversible capacities, ultralong cycle life, and superior rate performance.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, the detailed preparation procedure of the 3D H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite comprises a hydrothermal reaction, a carbon-coating process, phosphidation treatment, and an annealing process. Due to the hydrophobic nature of graphene, the anode materials with addition of graphene are favorable for the contact with the organic LiPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte. Surface wettability of a LiPF<sub>6</sub> droplet on the H-FeP, H-FeP@C@GR, and GR is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. The H-FeP membrane exhibited a large contact angle of 24.7° at the LiPF<sub>6</sub>/H-FeP interface, and this angle decreased slightly after 8 s, indicating its poor surface wettability. However, in the case of H-FeP@C@GR and GR membranes, wetting behaviors were significantly changed due to the existence of GR. The LiPF<sub>6</sub> droplet quickly spread out on the surface within 8 s, demonstrating its high hydrophobicity, which is beneficial for FeP contact with the nonpolar LiPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte. Moreover, high electrical conductivities were also demonstrated in the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite. As illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, due to the 3D omnibearing conductive networks from the GR skeleton and outer coating carbon, a higher electrical conductivity (1.4 × 10<sup>2</sup> S m<sup>–1</sup>) was obtained for the H-FeP@C@GR compared to those of pure H-FeP (4.8 × 10<sup>–3</sup> S m<sup>–1</sup>) and H-FeP@C (0.86 S m<sup>–1</sup>), which can impart greater reversibility to the reaction.</div><div class="NLM_p">SEM images in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a and b</a> demonstrated that the obtained H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanospheres as starting material were highly uniform with an average diameter of 300–400 nm. They had very high homogeneity and insignificant particle aggregation. The purity of H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanospheres was corroborated by X-ray diffraction (XRD) (JCPDS: 19-0629) in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. The surfaces of these nanospheres were uneven, clearly indicating that the nanospheres were assembled by ultrananoparticles driven by Ostwald ripening. The transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3c</a> confirmed that the nanospheres consisted of ultrananocrystals and displayed that they had a hollow nanostructure. According to the Scherrer equation based on XRD, the crystallite size was 4.1 nm. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a–c, it was seen that H-FeP, obtained by the phosphidation of H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, remained spherical in shape with high uniformity and without obvious aggregation. H-FeP nanospheres, the same as H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, were assembled by small nanocrystals, suggesting that phosphidation took place on a single H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> ultrananocrystal. The purity of H-FeP nanospheres was confirmed by XRD (JCPDS: 65-2595) in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. Based on the Scherrer equation, the H-FeP crystallite had a size of 9.6 nm, higher than that of H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, which might be due to the slight sintering in the phosphidation process. Meanwhile, the hollow nanostructure and self-assembly of H-FeP nanosphere were verified by TEM images in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a and b. An HRTEM image of H-FeP is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, which suggested that these small nanoparticles had sizes of tens of nanometers. The lattice-resolved TEM image taken over the area marked by a square in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c displayed a highly ordered fringe with a <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.251 nm, which was attributed to the (102) reflection of the FeP phase. Subsequently, as-prepared H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> product was carbonized by using glucose as a carbon source to prepare H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C. The success of this strategy could be confirmed by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and TEM images and XRD analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3d–f and S4</a>), where hollow Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> coated with carbon maintained well the hollow structure, spherical shape, and unchanged size. These resultant H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C nanospheres were then converted into H-FeP@C nanospheres <i>via</i> a solid/gas-phase phosphorization strategy. In this process, NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> was used as a precursor to generate PH<sub>3</sub> gas, which could react with the H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C nanospheres to form H-FeP@C nanospheres. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> SEM and TEM images (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d–f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure 3d</a>) showed that the spherical morphology was preserved after the phosphidation step. By observing the HRTEM image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f) magnified from the edge of the nanosphere in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, it was found that an obvious amorphous carbon layer was formed on the surface of the H-FeP nanosphere. The thickness of the carbon layer was around 4 nm. XRD of H-FeP@C in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a showed the high purity of the single FeP phase, and the crystallite size was 9.8 nm based on the Scherrer equation. Finally, the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite was obtained by mixing H-FeP@C with GR. The purity of the H-FeP nanospheres in the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite was confirmed by XRD in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>g and h, SEM and mapping images indicated that H-FeP nanospheres coated with thin carbon layers were incorporated into the GR matrix. In this process, GO was successfully transformed to GR, and the success of this strategy could be further confirmed by SEM images and XRD analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S5 and S6</a>), where layered structures, ruffled structures, and a clear layer spacing appearance of graphene-based sheets can be clearly observed. The EDX spectrum in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>i revealed that the atomic ratio of Fe:P was 1:1, matching with the XRD result. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>g and h exhibit that H-FeP nanospheres remained spherical in shape and that the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite maintained the integrity of a 3D nanostructure. The corresponding selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern clearly showed four bright diffraction rings, which could be indexed to (111), (202), (211), and (212) planes of orthorhombic FeP, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35, 36)</a> revealing the formation of polycrystalline FeP (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>i).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM images of (a–c) H-FeP nanospheres, (d–f) H-FeP@C nanospheres, and (g) the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite. (h) Element mapping images and (i) EDS spectrum of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. TEM and HRTEM images of (a–c) H-FeP nanospheres, (d–f) H-FeP@C nanospheres, and (g, h) the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite. (i) SAED patterns of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) XRD patterns and (b) TGA profiles of H-FeP nanospheres, H-FeP@C nanospheres, and the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite. (c) Raman spectra of H-FeP@C and the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to further evaluate the actual carbon content and its quality, thermal analysis and Raman spectroscopy were both performed. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, the small weight loss of about 2 wt % below 200 °C was ascribed to the removal of adsorbed water on the surface of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite, while the weight increase between 200 and 380 °C was attributed to the gradual oxidation of FeP to Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Finally, the drastic weight loss from 400 to 600 °C indicated that the carbon component in the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite was completely burned. By calculating the thermogravimetry analysis (TGA) results of H-FeP, H-FeP@C, and H-FeP@C@GR based on the oxidation reaction, the weight fractions were 67.6, 10.7, and 21.7 wt % for active material FeP, the coating carbon layer, and GR, respectively, in the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite. In the Raman spectra of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, two broad peaks located at 1355 and 1593 cm<sup>–1</sup> could be observed for the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite and H-FeP@C, corresponding to the sp<sup>3</sup>-type disordered carbon form (D band) and sp<sup>2</sup>-type ordered graphitic carbon form (G band), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 38 to 40">(38-40)</a> The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite was calculated to be 1.07, slightly higher than that of H-FeP@C 0.72, suggesting that the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite possessed more defects and disorders in the carbon component, which was favorable to improve the diffusion rate of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ions and electrons. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41, 42)</a> After making a complete literature survey, we find that the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite in our work is higher than those of the majority of double-carbon network modification anode materials reported in the literature, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 34 and 43 to 46">(34, 43-46)</a> which indicates that a carbon layer and graphene network with a certain number of defects and disorders in the H-FeP@C@GR could enhance the electrical and ionic transport significantly.</div><div class="NLM_p">XPS was employed to probe the surface electronic states and chemical composition of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite. The survey scan XPS spectrum (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a) revealed the presence of Fe, P, C, and O elements on the surface of the sample, which was consistent with the above mapping results. The high-resolution Fe 2p XPS spectrum is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. The peaks centered at 707.7 and 720.9 eV could be attributed to Fe (111) 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of FeP, while the peaks with binding energy values of 713.3 and 727.2 eV could be assigned to the oxidized form of Fe deriving from the prolonged exposure of H-FeP@C@GR to air. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref47" aria-label="reference 25 and 47">(25, 47)</a> For the high-resolution P 2p XPS spectrum (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c), two peaks at 129.3 and 130.1 eV corresponded to the binding energy of P 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and P 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively. The other P 2p peak located at 133.6 eV could be assigned to the partial surface oxidation of P species during the process of X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurement when the sample was exposed to ambient air. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref48" aria-label="reference 35 and 48">(35, 48)</a> The high-resolution XPS spectrum of C 1s (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d) was fitted with four components. In addition to the main peak located at 284.4 eV attributed to sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized graphitic C atoms and the peak at 285.6 eV ascribed to sp<sup>3</sup>-hybridized C atoms, the other two peaks at 286.4 and 288.8 eV were attributed to the functional groups on the GR sheets, such as C═O and O–C═O. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49, 50)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. XPS spectra of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite (a) survey and (b) Fe 2p, (c) P 2p, and (d) C 1s.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Electrochemical Characterizations for LIBs</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite was first investigated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the 0–3.0 V voltage range (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). An obvious difference between the first and the subsequent cycles was clearly seen. In the first cathodic sweep, an apparent wide reduction peak appeared at approximately 1.02 V, which could be attributed to the intercalation of Li<sup>+</sup> ions into FeP to form a Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP (<i>x</i> = 0–3) phase (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> The following peak at 0.44 V could be explained by the formation of an SEI film on the electrode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>) and the reduction of Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP into Fe and Li<sub>3</sub>P phases (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> The following anodic scan gave a broad oxidation peak at 1.08 V, which was related to the reverse processes of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>. In the subsequent cycles, it is noteworthy that the reduction peak shifted to 0.78 V while the oxidation peak stayed at the same position as in the first cycle, indicating an excellent reversible nature of the electrochemical reactions in the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite anode. For comparison, the CV result of the H-FeP electrode is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>, which apparently displayed that the current density decreased gradually from the first to the third cycle, indicating a relatively poor reversibility of the H-FeP electrode.<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/nn-2017-06625m_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/nn-2017-06625m_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/nn-2017-06625m_m003.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></span></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical performances of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite for LIBs. (a) Representative CV curves of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> within 0.005 and 3.0 V. (b) Selected discharge–charge curves for the initial three cycles of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Rate performance and (d) cycling performance of all the samples. (e) Long cycling performance of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The first three discharge–charge curves of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite anode at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> in a voltage window of 0.005–3 V are also shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b. The specific capacities were calculated based on the total mass of H-FeP@C and GR. In the first cycle, the H-FeP@C@GR electrode yielded an initial discharge and charge capacity of 1566 and 1154 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, with a Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 74%. These values were higher than those of H-FeP nanospheres (1269 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> with an initial CE of 66%) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>). The initial irreversible capacity losses for the two electrodes might be attributed to the formation of an SEI layer on the electrode surface and electrolyte decomposition, which occurs commonly in all anode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52 ref53 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 52 to 54">(52-54)</a> This characteristic was exactly consistent with the CV results showing that the reduction peaks, attributed to SEI formation, were present in the first cathodic sweep but absent afterward. In the subsequent two cycles, the specific capacities of H-FeP@C@GR exhibited only a slight decay, while the capacities of H-FeP decreased to a large extent. The excellent performance and high stability of the H-FeP@C@GR electrode clearly benefited from the introduction of coating carbon and encapsulated GR. Unexpectedly, no obvious plateaus at approximately 0.78 V for discharge and 1.08 V for charge, matching with CV results, were found in the discharge–charge curves, which might be due to the fact that the cathodic reaction of Li<sup>+</sup> ions into FeP to form a Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP (<i>x</i> = 0–3) phase at 0.78 V and the anodic reaction of delithiation of Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP (<i>x</i> = 0–3) to re-form FeP at 1.08 V were insufficiently active to build the noticeable plateaus in those curves. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref41" aria-label="reference 9 and 41">(9, 41)</a> The rate capabilities of all as-prepared electrodes from 0.2 to 8 A g<sup>–1</sup> were evaluated as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c. The H-FeP electrode delivered discharge capacities of 529 (10th), 221 (20th), 88 (30th), 42 (40th), 21 (50th), and 10 (60th) mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 4.0, and 8.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. By comparison, at the same rate, the corresponding discharge capacities for the H-FeP@C electrode were 859, 570, 441, 318, 222, and 187 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Notably, the discharge capacities of the two electrodes were unsatisfactory. In the case of the H-FeP@C@GR electrode, it offered obviously enhanced specific capacities of roughly 1030, 876, 755, 657, 577, and 482 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, demonstrating the architectural advantage of the 3D H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite for high-capacity and high-rate lithium storage. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d manifested the cycling performance of H-FeP, GR, H-FeP@C nanospheres, and the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. It can be seen that the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite greatly outperformed the H-FeP and H-FeP@C anodes in terms of cycling stability and capacity, which demonstrated that the construction of coating carbon and encapsulated GR could progressively optimize the electrochemical performance of the H-FeP nanospheres. Regarding the H-FeP electrode, its discharge capacity faded rapidly from 1270 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> to 207 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, which was primarily ascribed to the poor conductivity, serious aggregation, and pulverization of the H-FeP nanospheres during the cycling. Once the H-FeP nanospheres were coated with a carbon layer to form H-FeP@C, the electrode exhibited decent cycling stability with enhanced capacity (586 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles). In the case of H-FeP@C@GR, due to excellent electrical conductivity and 3D continuously interconnected macroporous structures of graphene with a large surface area, it could deliver a discharge capacity of 771 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which was approximately 3.7 times higher than that of H-FeP nanospheres. Moreover, the cycling performance of GR was also measured for comparison. The discharge capacity of GR was about 240 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles. By taking into account the low mass percent and low capacity of GR in the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite, it was concluded that GR contributed slightly to the lithium storage capacity of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite but played a crucial role in maintaining the structural integrity and enhancing the conductivity of the active materials. The long-term cycling performance of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> was also evaluated (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e). It was noted that the discharge capacity of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite was 678 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 50th cycle and remained at 542 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even after the 300th cycle. However, for the H-FeP@C nanospheres (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>), the corresponding specific capacities were 340 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 50th cycle and 115 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after the 200th cycle, respectively. The rapid capacity decay behavior of the H-FeP@C nanospheres could be attributed to the irreversible aggregation of the anospheres due to the absence of 3D continuously interconnected structures of GR, which markedly deteriorate full lithiation of their internal portions. To get insights into the differences in rate capabilities of H-FeP, H-FeP@C nanospheres, and the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) before and after the rate tests was carried out (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a and b). The EIS was fitted by an equivalent electrical circuit, as indicated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c. For the equivalent electrical circuit, the intercept of the high-frequency semicircle on the Z′ axis can be attributed to the resistance of the electrolyte (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>). The semicircle in the high- and middle-frequency regions respectively represent the SEI layer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub>) and charge-transfer impedance on the electrode–electrolyte interface (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), while the slope line at low frequency is related to the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>) of the lithium ion diffusion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> Those values were also collected in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. For the H-FeP@C@GR electrode, it is noted that there was a slight change in its <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> after the rate tests, demonstrating that the electrode had a stabilized SEI layer. By contrast, an obvious change in <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> could be observed for the H-FeP nanosphere anode. These results implied that repetitive breaks and growth of the SEI occurred during the rate tests of the H-FeP nanospheres, which acted as a barrier to hinder Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion and eventually resulted in poor rate capability. Like the <i>R</i><sub>f</sub>, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite was also slightly changed after the rate tests, thus further confirming the stability of the structure. As for H-FeP@C nanospheres, although the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> values showed small changes, the absence of 3D omnibearing conductive networks led to the aggregation of H-FeP nanospheres, inducing the destruction of electrical connections during prolonged cycling. The EIS results were in good agreement with the electrochemical behaviors of all the materials as anodes for LIBs.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Nyquist plots of H-FeP nanospheres, H-FeP@C nanospheres, and the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite (a) before and (b) after the rate tests. (c) Equivalent circuit used for fitting the experimental data.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">To further study the structural evolution and morphology change at different discharge–charge stages in LIBs, <i>ex-situ</i> TEM/HRTEM images and corresponding SAED patterns of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>. After charged in the 100th cycle to 3 V, shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a and b</a>, H-FeP@C@GR remained a spherical and porous structure without obvious aggregation, suggesting that a carbon and graphene matrix of nanostructured FeP nanospheres could effectively maintain structural stability for a very long cycling. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9c</a>, a few lattice fringes were observed with a <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.251 and 0.386 nm, assigned to the (102) and (101) planes of FeP (JCPDS: 65-2595), respectively. Furthermore, diffraction rings of FeP (111) and (211) were present in the SAED patterns shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9d</a>, consistent with <i>ex-situ</i> HRTEM, which proved complete delithiation of Li<sub>3</sub>P to re-form highly crystalline FeP nanocrystals. After initial discharge to 0.8 V, the HRTEM image in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9e</a> showed a few lattice fringes with a <i>d</i>-spacing at 0.380 nm, probably attributed to the Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP (x = 0–3) phase. Besides, the unclear patterns of the inset SAED might be due to the intermediate Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP phase. After full discharge to 0.005 V, in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9f</a>, lattice fringes with 0.209 nm spacing corresponded to the Li<sub>3</sub>P phase, and diffraction rings of SAED were consistent with Fe and Li<sub>3</sub>P phases.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Electrochemical Properties Analysis of SIBs</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Inspired by the significant lithium storage performance of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite, all the samples were also examined as anode materials for SIBs as a comparison. The electrochemical performance of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite was first investigated by CV at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the 0–3.0 V voltage range (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a).
