An obvious difference between the first and the subsequent cycles was clearly seen. In the first cathodic sweep, an apparent wide reduction peak appeared at approximately 0.59 V, which could be attributed to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into FeP to form a Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP (<i>x</i> = 0–3) phase. The following peak at 0.23 V could be explained by the formation of an SEI film on the electrode and the reduction of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP into Fe and Na<sub>3</sub>P phases. For the anodic curves, there were no apparent peaks, suggesting that in the whole oxidation process, the deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> from the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP (<i>x</i> = 0–3) phase to FeP was rather smooth without a fast reaction at a given voltage. In the subsequent cycles, it was noteworthy that the reduction peaks shifted to 1.0 V while the oxidation curves were overlapped, indicating an excellent reversible nature of the electrochemical reactions in the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite anode. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b shows the charge–discharge curves of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Clearly, the initial discharge and charge capacities achieved up to 995 and 656 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, with an initial CE of 66%. The large initial capacity loss was ascribed to the formation of irreversible Na<sub>2</sub>O and an SEI layer. In the subsequent cycles, the discharge and charge capacities of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite decreased slightly, further indicating its high capacity and excellent reversibility. In addition, in the discharge curves, there were small plateaus at around 1.16 V, matching the CV results. But no plateaus could be seen in the charge curves.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Electrochemical performances of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite for SIBs. (a) Representative CV curves of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> within 0.005 and 3.0 V. (b) Discharge–charge voltage profiles of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Rate performance and (d) long-term cycling property of the H-FeP@C@GR electrode at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (e) Nyquist plots of the H-FeP@C@GR electrode in LIBs and SIBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The rate capabilities of the three electrodes were evaluated at a series of current densities shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>c. Clearly, the discharge capacities of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite were 620 (10th), 487 (20th), 366 (30th), 285 (40th), and 237 (50th) mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.8, and 1.6 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. As the rate decreased back to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity could be as high as 480 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and remained stable in the following cycles. The discharge capacities of H-FeP@C and H-FeP at various current densities are listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>. Owing to structural features, the as-obtained H-FeP@C@GR electrode featured more superior cycle stability and higher rate capacity than the H-FeP@C and H-FeP electrodes. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>d shows the long-term cycling performance of the H-FeP@C@GR electrode at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. It was obvious that the capacity of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite faded significantly during the course of the first few cycles and showed a slight decay from the 40th cycle onward, whereas H-FeP nanospheres showed a continuous capacity decay to 141 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under the same conditions, revealed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>. After 250 cycles, the discharge capacity of the H-FeP@C@GR electrode was retained at 400 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. As for H-FeP@C, the first and the second discharge capacities were 943 and 633 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, whereas it was only 184 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). Clearly, the cycling stability and specific capacity of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite were higher than those of the H-FeP@C and H-FeP nanospheres, mainly due to the wrapping of GR that acted as a cage-like framework around the H-FeP@C nanospheres so as to prevent the agglomeration of active materials and cracking of the electrode. The reversible capacities of the H-FeP@C@GR electrode for SIBs were much lower than those of their counterparts in LIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>), which was because of the large radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (2.04 Å), unavoidably leading to a large volume change and poor kinetics of sodiation/desodiation processes upon cycling. This could be further confirmed by the comparison of the EIS spectra of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite in SIBs and LIBs. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>e, it is evident that the charge-transfer resistance of LIBs was much lower than that of SIBs, which was consistent with the electrochemical behaviors.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further study the structural evolution and morphology change at different discharge–charge stages in SIBs, <i>ex-situ</i> TEM/HRTEM images and corresponding SAED patterns of H-FeP@C@GR are displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>. After the H-FeP@C@GR was charged to 3 V in the 100th cycle, the <i>ex-situ</i> TEM image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>a) showed that it remained a spherical and porous structure and that the size was almost unchanged, suggesting that the carbon layer and graphene on nanostructured FeP can effectively maintain the structural stability for a very long cycling. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>b, a few lattice fringes were observed with <i>d</i>-spacings at 0.251 and 0.24 nm, assigned to the (102) and (101) planes of FeP (JCPDS: 65-2595), respectively. Moreover, diffraction rings of FeP (111), (202), (211), and (212) were present in the SAED patterns shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>c, consistent with <i>ex-situ</i> HRTEM, proving the complete desodiation of Na<sub>3</sub>P to re-form highly polycrystalline FeP nanocrystals. <i>Ex-situ</i> XRD and IR were also used to further investigate the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite after being charged to 3 V in the 100th cycle. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a</a>, <i>ex-situ</i> XRD pattern (I) indicated that the diffraction peaks at 47.0° and 48.4° could be assigned to the (202) and (211) plane of FeP (JCPDS: 65-2595), respectively, in addition to the strong peak of collector Ni, which corroborated that the Na<sub>3</sub>P was desodiated fully to re-form polycrystalline FeP nanocrystals. Moreover, in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11b</a>, <i>ex-situ</i> IR spectrum (I) showed that a broad absorption peak in the range of 500–700 cm<sup>–1</sup>, ascribed to a Fe–P vibration, was observed, consistent with the results above. Some other absorptions might be due to the existence of polymer binder in the sample. After it was initially discharged to 0.8 V, shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>d, <i>ex-situ</i> HRTEM of the nanosphere edge displayed that a few lattice planes were found with a <i>d</i>-spacing at 0.202 nm, corresponding to an Fe (002) plane, and at 0.308 and 0.430 nm, corresponding to (102) and (100) planes of Na<sub>3</sub>P, respectively, which elaborated that the edge portion of the FeP nanosphere was fully sodiated to form Fe and Na<sub>3</sub>P phases at this condition. Meanwhile, a single FeP nanosphere was characterized by SAED. The diffraction rings of Fe (101) and Na<sub>3</sub>P (102) and (110) were present in the SAED patterns shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>e, verifying the existence of Fe and Na<sub>3</sub>P phases. Another unknown diffraction ring might be due to the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP (<i>x</i> = 0–3) phase, which was mainly present in the internal portion of the FeP nanosphere. Likewise, after H-FeP@C@GR was initially discharged to 0.8 V, <i>ex-situ</i> XRD pattern (II) showed that the diffraction peak at 49.2° could be indexed to the (102) plane of Fe (JCPDS: 50-1275). From <i>ex</i>-IR spectrum (II) of <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11b</a>, the absorption peak could be attributed to the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP (<i>x</i> = 0–3) phase. Furthermore, after it was fully discharged to 0.005 V, shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>f and g, lattice planes matching with the Fe and Na<sub>3</sub>P phases were found. SAED patterns of a single nanosphere showed only Fe and Na<sub>3</sub>P phases without the presence of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP (<i>x</i> = 0–3), demonstrating the full sodiation of FeP, which is verified by the <i>ex-situ</i> XRD and IR in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>. Additionally, after the 100th cycle of charging to 3 V, the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite was analyzed by SEM and EDX, shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>. It can be seen that the FeP nanospheres were unchanged without collapse and aggregation in terms of shape, size, and hollow structure. From the EDX spectrum, the atomic ratio of Na:Fe:P was obtained as 0.26:1:1.07, implying that a certain amount of Na<sup>+</sup> ions was stored in the FeP nanocrystal without desodiation even after full charging, which might explain the capacity loss after cycling. More importantly, in the case of the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite, SEM equipped with energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) was used to investigate the amount of Na<sup>+</sup> ions intercalated into FeP nanocrystals, <i>i</i>.<i>e</i>., <i>x</i> variation in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP (<i>x</i> = 0–3), at different discharging voltages in its first discharge cycle, the result of which is displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig10" aria-label="Figure 10">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>. SEM images demonstrated that FeP nanospheres retained the spherical morphology and the same size after discharging. EDX spectra were collected from corresponding SEM images. The calculated atomic ratios of Na:Fe:P were 0.86:1:1.06, 2.09:1:1.07, and 2.97:1:1.07 at discharge voltages of 1.5, 0.8, and 0.005 V, respectively. The curve of the voltage as a function of <i>x</i> variation in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeP (<i>x</i> = 0–3) was plotted, the trend of which was consistent with that of the discharge curve.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. <i>Ex-situ</i> TEM/HRTEM images and corresponding SAED patterns of the H-FeP@C@GR at different discharge–charge stages in SIBs: (a–c) 100th charge to 3 V, (d, e) initial discharge to 0.8 V, and (f, g) initial discharge to 0.005 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig10" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0010.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. <i>Ex-situ</i> SEM and EDX spectra of the H-FeP@C@GR electrode collected at various points in SIBs: (a) after first discharging to 1.5 V, (b) after first discharging to 0.8 V, and (c) after first discharging to 0.005 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">From the above assessments, it has been established that H-FeP@C@GR showed more enhanced specific capacity, rate capability, and cycling stability in LIBs than in SIBs, which is due to more sluggish kinetics occurring in insertion/extraction of SIBs than that of LIBs. To demonstrate this, we carried out density functional theory (DFT) calculations and simulated the adsorption of Li<sup>+</sup> ions and Na<sup>+</sup> ions on the FeP surface as well as Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion in FeP. By comparing all the possible adsorption sites, we concluded that the adsorptions of Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> ions at the sites of P atoms were the most energetically stable. The Li<sup>+</sup> ion adsorption energy was calculated to be 0.21 eV, while the Na<sup>+</sup> ion adsorption energy was calculated to be −0.12 eV, indicating that Na-FeP adsorption interactions were more favorable than Li-FeP interactions, which could be explained by the fact that Na has more electrons and a larger radius than Li. Additionally, the simulation of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion in an FeP crystal was carried out. To simulate the diffusion processes of Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> in bulk FeP, first, a 2<i>a</i> × <i>b</i> × 2<i>c</i> supercell of orthorhombic FeP (space group <i>Pnma</i>(62)) was built using the experimental parameters, and one Li impurity was interstitially doped into it to find the most stable doped position. We considered the system of Li<sup>+</sup> located on the most stable doped position as a reactant and the system of Li<sup>+</sup> located on the neighboring equivalent position as a product. Ten images were inserted between the reactant and product, and the climbing image scheme was used to search the transient state. Moreover, to compare the diffusion process of Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> in bulk FeP, we computed the diffusion process of Na<sup>+</sup> using the diffusion path of the Li<sup>+</sup>. As schematically shown in the inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig11" aria-label="Figure 11">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">11</a>a and b, the diffusion barrier energy of the lithium ion in the optimized process was 11.133 eV, while the diffusion barrier energy of the sodium ion was 16.711 eV. This indicated that the Li<sup>+</sup> ion with smaller diffusion barrier energy can achieve faster charge and discharge than the Na<sup>+</sup> ion, consequently boosting the power rate performance of the lithium ion batteries. Meanwhile, our DFT calculations showed that the large and advantageous cavity structure of bulk FeP were more favorable for the storage of lithium ions. On the basis of the above simulation, we can see that compared to the high diffusion barrier energy (11.133 eV for Li<sup>+</sup> and 16.711 eV for Na<sup>+</sup>), the adsorption energy (0.21 eV for Li<sup>+</sup> and −0.12 eV for Na<sup>+</sup>) could be neglected, suggesting that ion diffusion barrier energy was a much more crucial parameter for the battery performance. Overall, the diffusion barrier energy of Li<sup>+</sup> was lower than that of Na<sup>+</sup>, leading to FeP demonstrating more excellent performance in LIBs than in SIBs, matching with the experimental results.</div><figure id="fig11" data-index="11" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 11</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-06625m_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0011.jpeg" id="gr11" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 11. Diffusion barrier energy (Δ<i>E</i>) of Li<sup>+</sup> ions (a) and Na<sup>+</sup> ions (b) along the optimized path in the bulk FeP.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/asset/images/large/nn-2017-06625m_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b06625&amp;id=fig11"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, we successfully designed a 3D GR encapsulated with a hollow FeP@carbon nanocomposite <i>via</i> a combination of hydrothermal reaction, carbon coating process, phosphidation treatment, and carbothermic reaction. SEM and TEM confirmed that H-FeP@C nanospheres were strongly anchored on the surface of the stacked structure as well as in the parallel layers of the GR. This 3D network structure can not only naturally accommodate the lithiation/sodiation-induced volume change but also offer more active sites for lithium/sodium ion insertion and electron pathways for electron and ion transportation. As a result, when tested in LIBs and SIBs, the H-FeP@C@GR electrode yielded an excellent electrochemical performance in terms of reversibility, rate capacity, and cycling performance. For example, the H-FeP@C@GR electrode showed a specific capacity about 771 and 446 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles for LIBs and SIBs (much higher than those of H-FeP and H-FeP@C), respectively. Besides, the H-FeP@C@GR electrode could also afford a high current density. When the H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite was used as an anode material for LIBs at different current densities in charge/discharge processes (0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 4, and 8 A g<sup>–1</sup>), the capacity still can be recovered to 953 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec6_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Materials</h3><div id="sec22" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Synthesis of Hollow Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) Nanospheres</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The nanospheres were synthesized by a solvothermal method. Briefly, 2.70 g of FeCl<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O and 7.20 g of sodium acetate were dissolved in 100 mL of ethylene glycol under magnetic stirring. After stirring for 1 h, the resulting homogeneous yellow solution was transferred to a Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave. The autoclave was heated at 200 °C for 8 h and then naturally cooled to ambient temperature. Finally, the resulting H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanospheres were washed seven times with ethanol and dried under vacuum at 80 °C for 10 h.</div></div><div id="sec23" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Synthesis of H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C Nanospheres</h4><div class="NLM_p last">Typically, 0.05 g of as-synthesized H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and 0.5 g of glucose were dissolved in a solution containing 17.5 mL of deionized water and 5 mL of ethanol followed by vigorous stirring for 30 min. Then, the solution was placed in a 50 mL Teflon-sealed autoclave and maintained at 180 °C for 2 h. The precipitates were centrifuged and washed with deionized water and ethanol and dried under vacuum at 80 °C for 10 h. Finally, the resulting samples were calcined at 500 °C for 4 h in flowing argon.</div></div><div id="sec24" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Synthesis of H-FeP and H-FeP@C Nanospheres</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The H-FeP nanospheres were fabricated by the phosphidation of H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. In a typical procedure, the obtained H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanospheres and NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> were placed at two separate positions in one closed porcelain crucible with NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> at the upstream side of the furnace. The weight ratio of the H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanospheres to NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> was 1:20. Subsequently, the samples were heated at 300 °C for 3 h with a heating speed of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup> in a static Ar atmosphere. Last, the black H-FeP nanospheres were obtained after cooling to ambient temperature under Ar flow. The H-FeP@C nanospheres were synthesized by the same procedure using H-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C nanospheres as starting material.</div></div><div id="sec21" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Synthesis of 3D H-FeP@C@GR Nanocomposite</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite was prepared through direct annealing of H-FeP@C nanospheres that were simply mixed with GO sheets obtained by our previous work. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> Typically, 80 mg of H-FeP@C nanospheres were first dispersed in 100 mL of distilled water and ultrasonically treated for 2 h. Then, 20 mL of homogeneous GO (7.5 mg mL<sup>–1</sup>) aqueous suspension was added into the H-FeP@C nanosphere suspension under magnetic stirring for 6 h. After that, the mixed suspension was collected by freeze-drying and then annealed at 600 °C for 2 h in an Ar atmosphere to finally obtain the 3D H-FeP@C@GR nanocomposite.</div></div></div><div id="sec6_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The phase purity and crystal structure of samples were measured by a Bruker D8 ADVANCE X-ray powder diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 0.154 18 nm) at a scanning rate of 0.02° s<sup>–1</sup> in the 2θ range from 10° to 80°. SEM images of products were obtained using a FEI Quanta 400 ESEM-FEG instrument with an accelerating voltage of 20 kV. TEM images and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images were obtained by a JEOL JEM-3010 instrument. The XPS experiments were carried out on a PHI-5400 electron spectrometer. The Raman spectrum was performed by a Raman spectrometer with 532 nm laser excitation. TGA of the product was conducted from room temperature up to 800 °C with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> under flowing air (TGA, Q 600). Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra were recorded on a Bruker Optics Tensor 27 FT-IR spectrometer (Germany).</div></div><div id="sec6_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical behavior was performed with 2025 coin-type cells assembled in a glovebox in an argon atmosphere. The working electrode was prepared by mixing active materials (80 wt %) with acetylene black (10 wt %) and polytetrafluoroethylene (PVDF, 10 wt %) to form a slurry. The obtained slurry was spread uniformly on a circular piece of nickel foam with 14 mm diameter. The nickel foam was pressed at 20 MPa so as to obtain good contact between the nickel foam and then dried at 80 °C in a vacuum oven for 12 h. The active material loading in each electrode was about 1.2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The electrolyte involves LiPF<sub>6</sub> (1 M) dissolved in a mixture of dimethyl carbonate, diethyl carbonate, and ethylene carbonate (1:1:1 by volume), and the separator was microporous polypropylene film. As for sodium batteries, the electrolyte consisted of a solution of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate (1:1 by volume). The charge–discharge tests were carried out on a LAND battery program-control test system in a cutoff potential window of 0–3.0 V after aging for more than 10 h. The CV between 0.005 and 3.0 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> and EIS with the frequency range from 0.01 to 100 kHz were carried out on a CHI 660D electrochemical workstation.</div></div><div id="sec6_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14"> Computational Details</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To simulate the diffusion process of Li<sup>+</sup> in bulk FeP, the climbing image nudged elastic band method was used. All calculations were performed based on the DFT within the open-source code Quantum ESPRESSO, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> with the GBRV ultrasoft pseudopotentials <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> to describe the electron–ionic core interaction. We adopted the generalized gradient approximation of Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof for the solid <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> to describe the exchange–correlation interaction of electrons, and the wave functions were expanded in a plane-wave basis set with an energy cutoff of 40 Ry to ensure accurate results. The force on each ion was converged to be less than 0.001 Ry/au, and all the geometric structures were fully relaxed to minimize the total energy of the system until a precision of 10<sup>–4</sup> Ry was reached. The Fe 3s<sup>2</sup>3p<sup>6</sup>3d<sup>6</sup>4s<sup>2</sup>, P 3s<sup>2</sup>3p<sup>3</sup>, and Li 1s<sup>2</sup>2s<sup>1</sup> electrons were treated as valence electrons. A 2 × 4 × 4 k-point grid in reciprocal space was used to ensure the convergence for the total energy self-consistent calculations.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i15">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsnano.7b06625</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Characterizations of XRD, SEM, TEM, HRTEM, SAED, surface wettability, and electrical conductivities for prepared materials; electrochemical measurements including CV curves, cycling performances, and rate capabilities of FeP-based materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b06625/suppl_file/nn7b06625_si_001.pdf">nn7b06625_si_001.pdf (4.01 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.7b06625" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Graphene Quantum Dots Coated VO<sub>2</sub> Arrays for Highly Durable Electrodes for Li and Na Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">15</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">565</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">573</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/nl504038s</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D15%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D565-573%26journal%3DNano%2BLett.%26author%3DD.%2BL.%2BChao%26author%3DC.%2BR.%2BZhu%26author%3DX.%2BH.%2BXia%26author%3DJ.%2BL.%2BLiu%26author%3DX.%2BZhang%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DP.%2BLiang%26author%3DJ.%2BY.%2BLin%26author%3DH.%2BZhang%26author%3DZ.%2BX.%2BShen%26title%3DGraphene%2BQuantum%2BDots%2BCoated%2BVO2%2BArrays%2Bfor%2BHighly%2BDurable%2BElectrodes%2Bfor%2BLi%2Band%2BNa%2BIon%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fnl504038s&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.7b06625&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fnl504038s&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Graphene Quantum Dots Coated VO2 Arrays for Highly Durable Electrodes for Li and Na Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chao, Dongliang; Zhu, Changrong; Xia, Xinhui; Liu, Jilei; Zhang, Xiao; Wang, Jin; Liang, Pei; Lin, Jianyi; Zhang, Hua; Shen, Ze Xiang; Fan, Hong Jin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">565-573</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nanoscale surface engineering is playing important role in enhancing the performance of battery electrode.  VO2 is one of high-capacity but less-stable materials and has been used mostly in the form of powders for Li-ion battery cathode with mediocre performance.  In this work, we design a new type of binder-free cathode by bottom-up growth of bifacial VO2 arrays directly on a graphene network for both high-performance Li-ion and Na-ion battery cathodes.  More importantly, graphene quantum dots are coated onto the VO2 surfaces as a highly efficient surface sensitizer and protection to further boost the electrochem. properties.  The integrated electrodes deliver a Na storage capacity of 306 mA-h/g at 100 mA/g, and a capacity of more than 110 mA-h/g after 1500 cycles at 18 A/g.  Our result on Na-ion battery may pave the way to next generation post-lithium batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqlNChWsQ5gnLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljNBxt5z6uDxg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitFehsL7L&amp;md5=64c5ad4c1680e984a714a38620297d09</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA07452A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X. Mater. Chem. A</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">4</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">18532</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">18542</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">One-dimensional hybrid nanocomposite of high-density monodispersed Fe3O4 nanoparticles and carbon nanotubes for high-capacity storage of lithium and sodium</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Xiujuan; Liu, Xiaojie; Wang, Gang; Xia, Yuan; Wang, Hui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">47</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">18532-18542</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Here we report a simple but effective method to fill carbon nanotube (CNT) channels with a dense distribution of Fe3O4 nanoparticles, to produce Fe3O4@CNT.  The resulting novel heterogeneous nanostructure displayed multiple attractive features as an anode material in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  For example, this nanostructure displayed voids that apparently effectively prevented active material from aggregating and helped to accommodate their volumetric expansion during the cycling process.  In addn., most solid electrolyte interphases (SEIs) formed on the surfaces of the CNT shells instead of on individual Fe3O4 nanoparticles, limiting the amt. of SEIs and increasing the stability of the Fe3O4 nanoparticles.  As a result, when tested for LIBs, the Fe3O4@CNT nanocomposite exhibited an excellent rate capacity and cycling performance in half and full cells.  A high reversible capacity of 720 mA h g-1 was obtained after 200 cycles at 1 A g-1, and when the c.d. was increased to 8 A g-1, the specific capacity was 400 mA h g-1.  As for SIBs, a specific capacity of 377 mA h g-1 at 0.1 A g-1 was achieved after 300 cycles.  The facile synthetic route and unique nanostructure design may be extended to high-performance anode materials for LIBs and SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpPJRClqmvTG7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljNBxt5z6uDxg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhslGgsbrK&amp;md5=e63c2974a1c64aa5ee40bb0393e20173</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2013.03.019"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fu, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xue, L. G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yildiz, O.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, G. W.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Effect of CVD Carbon Coatings on Si@CNF Composite as Anode for Lithium-ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Energy</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">2</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">976</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">986</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.nanoen.2013.03.019</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D2%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D976-986%26journal%3DNano%2BEnergy%26author%3DK.%2BFu%26author%3DL.%2BG.%2BXue%26author%3DO.%2BYildiz%26author%3DS.%2BLi%26author%3DH.%2BLee%26author%3DY.%2BLi%26author%3DG.%2BJ.%2BXu%26author%3DL.%2BZhou%26author%3DP.%2BD.%2BBradford%26author%3DX.%2BW.%2BZhang%26title%3DEffect%2Bof%2BCVD%2BCarbon%2BCoatings%2Bon%2BSi%2540CNF%2BComposite%2Bas%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BLithium-ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.nanoen.2013.03.019&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.7b06625&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.nanoen.2013.03.019&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Effect of CVD carbon coatings on Si@CNF composite as anode for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fu, Kun; Xue, Leigang; Yildiz, Ozkan; Li, Shuli; Lee, Hun; Li, Ying; Xu, Guanjie; Zhou, Lan; Bradford, Philip D.; Zhang, Xiangwu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">976-986</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-ion battery (LIB) anodes with high capacity and binder free structure were synthesized from carbon nanofibers that contained silicon nanoparticles (Si@CNF).  The particle filled nonwoven structures were produced by an electrospinning and subsequent carbonization process.  Pristine Si@CNF composites had Si nanoparticles exposed on the fiber surface.  As produced, the Si nanoparticles could become detached from the nanofiber surface during cycling, causing severe structural damage and capacity loss.  In order to prevent Si from detaching from the nanofiber surface, the Si@CNF composite was then treated with a thermal chem. vapor deposition (CVD) technique to make Si completely coated with a carbon matrix.  The carbon coated Si@CNF (Si@CNF-C) composites were synthesized with different Si contents (10, 30, and 50 wt%) for different CVD treatment times (30, 60, and 90 min).  It was found that the initial coulombic efficiency of Si@CNF-C could be increased via the amorphous carbon by stabilizing solid-electrolyte-interface (SEI) formation on surface.  The capacity and cyclic stability were improved by the CVD carbon coating, esp. for the 30 wt% Si@CNF-C composite with 90 min CVD coating, a CVD amorphous carbon coating of less than 1% by wt. on Si@CNF composites contributed to more than 200% improvement in cycling performance.  Results indicate that the CVD carbon coating is an effective approach to improve the electrochem. properties of Si@CNF composites making this a potential route to obtain high-energy d. anode materials for LIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqBBMJMVkR_crVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liSSco5nHJahw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXnt1Siu7c%253D&amp;md5=0dce47d324ae29daece33fb47d8e3109</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C5NR01145K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hwang, S. H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrospun Manganese-Cobalt Oxide Hollow Nanofibres Synthesized Combustion Reactions and Their Lithium Storage Performance</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nanoscale</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">7</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">8351</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">8355</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

P.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Loading Nano-SnO<sub>2</sub> Encapsulated <i>in Situ</i> in Three-Dimensional Rigid Porous Carbon for Superior Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem. - Eur. J.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">22</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">4915</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">4923</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/chem.201504420</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D22%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D4915-4923%26journal%3DChem.%2B-%2BEur.%2BJ.%26author%3DH.%2BR.%2BXue%26author%3DJ.%2BQ.%2BZhao%26author%3DJ.%2BTang%26author%3DH.%2BGong%26author%3DP.%2BHe%26author%3DH.%2BS.%2BZhou%26author%3DY.%2BYamauchi%26author%3DJ.%2BP.%2BHe%26title%3DHigh-Loading%2BNano-SnO2%2BEncapsulated%2Bin%2BSitu%2Bin%2BThree-Dimensional%2BRigid%2BPorous%2BCarbon%2Bfor%2BSuperior%2BLithium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fchem.201504420&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.7b06625&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fchem.201504420&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Loading Nano-SnO2 Encapsulated in situ in Three-Dimensional Rigid Porous Carbon for Superior Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xue, Hairong; Zhao, Jianqing; Tang, Jing; Gong, Hao; He, Ping; Zhou, Haoshen; Yamauchi, Yusuke; He, Jianping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - A European Journal</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">22</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4915-4923</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CEUJED</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0947-6539</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin oxide nanoparticles (SnO2 NPs) have been encapsulated in situ in a three-dimensional ordered space structure.  Within this composite, ordered mesoporous carbon (OMC) acts as a carbon framework showing a desirable ordered mesoporous structure with an av. pore size (≈6 nm) and a high surface area (470.3 m2/g), and the SnO2 NPs (≈10 nm) are highly loaded (up to 80 wt %) and homogeneously distributed within the OMC matrix.  As an anode material for lithium-ion batteries, a SnO2@OMC composite material can deliver an initial charge capacity of 943 mAh/g and retain 68.9 % of the initial capacity after 50 cycles at a c.d. of 50 mA/g, even exhibit a capacity of 503 mA h/g after 100 cycles at 160 mA/g.  In situ encapsulation of the SnO2 NPs within an OMC framework contributes to a higher capacity and a better cycling stability and rate capability in comparison with bare OMC and OMC ex situ loaded with SnO2 particles (SnO2/OMC).  The significantly improved electrochem. performance of the SnO2@OMC composite can be attributed to the multifunctional OMC matrix, which can facilitate electrolyte infiltration, accelerate charge transfer, and lithium-ion diffusion, and act as a favorable buffer to release reaction strains for lithiation/delithiation of the SnO2 NPs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqxWmCusogv67Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liSSco5nHJahw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XjtlKgurs%253D&amp;md5=0d2ea19484023c4ed37b0940eeb61a77</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.6b07450"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zuo, X. Funct. Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">26</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">5019</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">5027</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adfm.201601323</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D26%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D5019-5027%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct.%2BMater.%26author%3DM.%2BP.%2BFan%26author%3DY.%2BChen%26author%3DY.%2BH.%2BXie%26author%3DT.%2BZ.%2BYang%26author%3DX.%2BW.%2BShen%26author%3DN.%2BXu%26author%3DH.%2BY.%2BYu%26author%3DC.%2BL.%2BYan%26title%3DHalf-Cell%2Band%2BFull-Cell%2BApplications%2Bof%2BHighly%2BStable%2Band%2BBinder-Free%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%2BBased%2Bon%2BCu3P%2BNanowire%2BAnodes%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.201601323&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.7b06625&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.201601323&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Half-Cell and Full-Cell Applications of Highly Stable and Binder-Free Sodium Ion Batteries Based on Cu3P Nanowire Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Mouping; Chen, Yu; Xie, Yihao; Yang, Tingzhou; Shen, Xiaowei; Xu, Na; Yu, Haiying; Yan, Chenglin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">28</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5019-5027</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion battery (SIB) is esp. attractive in cost-effective energy storage device as an alternative to lithium-ion battery.  Particularly, metal phosphides as potential anodes for SIBs have recently been demonstrated owing to their higher specific capacities compared with those of carbonaceous materials.  Unfortunately, most reported metal phosphides consist of irregular particles ranged from several hundreds nanometers to tens of micrometers, thus delivering limited cyclic stability.  This paper reports the sodium storage properties of additive-free Cu3P nanowire (CPNW) anode directly grown on copper current collector via an in situ growth followed by phosphidation method.  Therefore, as a result of its structure features, CPNW anode demonstrates highly stable cycling ability with an ≈70% retention in capacity at the 260th cycle, whereas most reported metal phosphides have limited cycle nos. ranged between 30 and 150.  Besides, the reaction mechanism between Cu3P and Na is investigated by examg. the intermediate products at different charge/discharge stages using ex situ X-ray diffraction measurements.  Furthermore, to explore the practical application of CPNW anode, a pouch-type Na+ full cell consisting of CPNW anode and Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode is assembled and characterized.  As a demonstration, a 10 cm × 10 cm light-emitting diode (LED) screen is successfully powered by the Na+ full cell.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqJuwM8_tSmJLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liSSco5nHJahw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XosVSlt74%253D&amp;md5=88fc2ecb8a32e8ed2bff3659fbc36cff</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1039/C6NR01774F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, P. P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qin, J. W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J. Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, M. H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Three-Dimensional Porous MoP@C Hybrid as a High-Capacity, Long-Cycle Life Anode Material for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nanoscale</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">8</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">10330</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">10338</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C6NR01774F</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D8%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D10330-10338%26journal%3DNanoscale%26author%3DX.%2BWang%26author%3DP.%2BP.%2BSun%26author%3DJ.%2BW.%2BQin%26author%3DJ.%2BQ.%2BWang%26author%3DY.%2BXiao%26author%3DM.%2BH.%2BCao%26title%3DA%2BThree-Dimensional%2BPorous%2BMoP%2540C%2BHybrid%2Bas%2Ba%2BHigh-Capacity%252C%2BLong-Cycle%2BLife%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BLithium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC6NR01774F&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.7b06625&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC6NR01774F&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A three-dimensional porous MoP@C hybrid as a high-capacity, long-cycle life anode material for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Xia; Sun, Pingping; Qin, Jinwen; Wang, Jianqiang; Xiao, Ying; Cao, Minhua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">19</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10330-10338</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NANOHL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2040-3372</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal phosphides are great promising anode materials for lithium-ion batteries with a high gravimetric capacity.  However, significant challenges such as low capacity, fast capacity fading and poor cycle stability must be addressed for their practical applications.  Herein, we demonstrate a versatile strategy for the synthesis of a novel three-dimensional porous molybdenum phosphide@carbon hybrid (3D porous MoP@C hybrid) by a template sol-gel method followed by an annealing treatment.  The resultant hybrid exhibits a 3D interconnected ordered porous structure with a relatively high surface area.  Benefiting from its advantages of microstructure and compn., the 3D porous MoP@C hybrid displays excellent lithium storage performance as an anode material for lithium-ion batteries in terms of specific capacity, cycling stability and long-cycle life.  It presents stable cycling performance with a high reversible capacity up to 1028 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 after 100 cycles.  By ex situ XRD, HRTEM, SAED and XPS analyses, the 3D porous MoP@C hybrid was found to follow the Li-intercalation reaction mechanism (MoP + xLi+ + e- ↔ LixMoP), which was further confirmed by ab initio calcns. based on d. functional theory.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoWT3_ZjreS2bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljuTsxJ08BLEA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xms1Wgt7w%253D&amp;md5=c20b89d86f145fa380ddb1f28cd91258</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2012.12.037"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cui, Y. H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xue, M. Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fu, Z. W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X. L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanocrystalline CoP thin film as a new anode material for lithium ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cui, Yan-Hua; Xue, Ming-Zhe; Fu, Zheng-Wen; Wang, Xiao-Ling; Liu, Xiao-Jiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nanocryst. CoP thin film was prepd. by pulsed laser deposition and the electrochem. investigation of CoP as anode material for lithium-ion battery was reported.  The reversible discharge capacity of CoP/Li cell cycled between 0.1-3.0 V was found in the range of 788.0-1055.7 mA-h/g during the first 25 cycles.  A reversible couple of redox peaks at 0.63 and 1.02 V was obsd. in the first cycle of cyclic voltammetry curves.  Ex situ transmission electron microscopy and selected-area electron diffraction revealed the complete decompn. of CoP to Co/Li3P composite after discharging the CoP/Li cell to 0.1 V and the re-formation of CoP nanocryst. structure after recharging to 3.0 V, demonstrating a fully reversible conversion reaction mechanism between CoP and Co/Li3P composite.  High utility of trivalent phosphorus benefits the reversibility and capacity of CoP and makes it potential anode material for future lithium-ion battery.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq2cuf0bicQWrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljuTsxJ08BLEA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXisVOqsrg%253D&amp;md5=6c67c34e1e46bfa1514100f6baf69a70</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201604159"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mei, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pramanik, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ide, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Alothman, Z.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly Ordered Mesostructured Vanadium Phosphonate toward Electrode Materials for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mei, Peng; Pramanik, Malay; Lee, Jaewoo; Ide, Yusuke; Alothman, Zeid Abdullah; Kim, Jung Ho; Yamauchi, Yusuke</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - A European Journal</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">23</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">18</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Highly ordered mesostructured vanadium phosphonates (VP) were synthesized in the presence of cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) as a structure-directing agent.  Nitrilotris(methylene)triphosphonic acid (NMPA) and (ammonium/sodium) metavanadate (NH4VO3/NaVO3) were used for the construction of pore walls.  The CTAB templates are removed from the materials by an extn. process without destroying the parent mesostructure.  The formation mechanism for the ordered mesoporous structure and its impact on electrochem. application in lithium ion batteries (LIBs) are explained by considering the structural and electrochem. stability of the framework.  The results demonstrate that the counter cations (NH4+/Na+) of the metavanadate precursors have a crucial role in stabilizing the mesoporous structure of the mesoporous VP materials.  Mesoporous VP materials with highly ordered structure have great applicability as high-performance electrode materials in LIBs due to the advantages of their large contact area with electrolyte and short transport paths for lithium ions.  Mesoporous VP electrodes exhibit high reversible specific capacity with superb cycling stability (100 cycles) and excellent retention of capacity (92 %).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq0SWZQQDMxUbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljuTsxJ08BLEA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXktVeqsrs%253D&amp;md5=cda45714166a834764ed0dae1b0fa6fb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1021/cm800632f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Park, C.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tetragonal Zinc Diphosphide and Its Nanocomposite as an Anode for Lithium Secondary Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Park, Cheol-Min; Sohn, Hun-Joon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Tetragonal zinc diphosphide (ZnP2) and its nanocomposite (ZnP2/C) were prepd. using com. available amorphous red phosphorus and zinc powder by high energy mech. milling at ambient temp. and pressure.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Well-dispersed and porous FeP@C nanoplates with stable and ultrafast lithium storage performance through conversion reaction mechanism</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Fei; Zhang, Chengzhi; Yang, Jianxiao; Ma, Guozhi; He, Kejian; Li, Xuanke</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12781-12789</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Conformally carbon-coated FeP (FeP@C) nanoplates with a porous feature are synthesized by integrating three simple approaches: hydrothermal growth of Fe2O3 nanoplates, the spatial introduction of conformal carbon coating, and chem. conversion of an intermediate into FeP by a low-temp. phosphidation strategy.  The introduction of carbon coating not only serves as a buttering to effectively prevent the restacking of nanoplates, but also largely improves elec. cond. and reinforces the structural robustness of FeP.  The obtained FeP@C nanoplates show two-dimensional morphol. and possess a large surface-area-to-vol.-ratio and abundant inner mesopores with a high sp. surface area.  As an anode of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), the prepd. FeP@C nanoplates exhibit exceptionally high electrochem. performance, which largely outperforms the Fe3O4@C counterpart under identical morphol. properties in exptl. and simulated results.  As a result, the FeP@C nanoplates exhibit a reversible specific capacity of 720 mA h g-1 and a capacity retention rate of 96% after 100 cycles at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1.  Even at a higher c.d. of 500 mA g-1, it still delivers a stable capacity of 610 mA h g-1 within 400 cycles with negligible capacity fading, which opens venues for long life-time applications of LIBs.  Furthermore, the superior rate capability of such anodes is also obtained with a reversible specific capacity of 347 mA h g-1 at 5000 mA g-1.  The outstanding lithium ion storage properties of the FeP@C nanoplates may be attributed to the reduced energy barrier for the Li storage reaction, shortened electronic/ionic transfer paths, and the phys. buffering effect rooted in the rational integration of the phosphide-based species, plate-like nanostructure and conformal carbon surface modification.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqjXW6_aelAGrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh7OX_i7J51gg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtFegu7jP&amp;md5=26d156be0b7d79fe308f8e64d048ac91</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1039/C4CC09604E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, W.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Mesoporous Iron Phosphonate Electrodes with Crystalline Frameworks for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pramanik, Malay; Tsujimoto, Yoshihiro; Malgras, Victor; Dou, Shi Xue; Kim, Jung Ho; Yamauchi, Yusuke</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A new family of mesoporous iron phosphonate (FeP) materials has been prepd. through cooperative assembly of cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB), iron nitrate, and nitrilotris(methylene)triphosphonic acid (NMPA).  CTAB is used as a structure directing agent, while the other two chems. are used as precursors for the formation of pore walls.  An extn. procedure is employed to remove the template without damaging the as-prepd. ordered mesostructure.  The obtained mesoporous FeP materials are well characterized by low angle X-ray diffraction (XRD), N2 adsorption isotherms, and transmission electron microscopy.  The mesostructural ordering of the obtained materials strongly depends on the synthetic conditions.  The morphol. and the crystallinity of the pore walls are investigated by SEM and wide-angle XRD measurements, resp.  It is revealed that the FeP framework is crystd. in the tetragonal crystal phase (I41/amd), according to the Rietveld refinement of the XRD patterns through the MAUD program.  The unit cell parameters of the obtained crystals are a = b = 5.1963 (3) Å, c = 12.9808 (1) Å (α = β = γ = 90°).  Also, the homogeneous distribution of both Fe species and organo-phosphonic acid groups in the mesoporous architectures is confirmed by Fourier transform IR spectroscopy and elemental mapping.  Mesoporous FeP materials with high surface area have great applicability as high performance electrode materials for lithium-ion (Li-ion) batteries, due to several advantages including a large contact area with the electrolyte, high structural stability, and short transport paths for Li+ ions.  Mesoporous FeP electrodes exhibit high reversible specific capacity with very good cycling stability and excellent retention of capacity.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrC8oCzwWQrULVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh7OX_i7J51gg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVOiur0%253D&amp;md5=ba8e0e4dfe92c692a0da164b2e3a0041</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1021/cm060433m"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Boyanov, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bernardi, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gillot, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dupont, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Womes, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">FeP: Another Attractive Anode for the Li-Ion Battery Enlisting a Reversible Two-Step Insertion/Conversion Process</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Boyanov, S.; Bernardi, J.; Gillot, F.; Dupont, L.; Womes, M.; Tarascon, J.-M.; Monconduit, L.; Doublet, M.-L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">FePy (y = 1, 2, 4) anodes all react with lithium through a conversion reaction FePy + 3yLi → yLi3P + Fe0 in their first discharge, leading to nanocomposite discharged electrodes described by nanosized Fe0 particles embedded in yLi3P matrixes.  From electrochem. and complementary in situ X-ray diffraction and high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy studies, the conversion reaction occurring during the first discharge is followed by two successive insertion and conversion processes in further cycles for the FeP electrode.  The insertion process is highly reversible, leading to a capacity retention of 300 mA h/g and 1900 mA h/cm3 after 100 cycles, and corresponds to the formation of an intermediate tetragonal LiFeP phase as deduced from first-principles T = 0 K phase diagram calcns. and preliminary Moessbauer analyses.  The kinetics of this reaction was expected to be strongly limited by the increase in y, thus leading to an increasing capacity fading when increasing the y P/Fe ratio.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqyBNQ0_ohOebVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh7OX_i7J51gg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD28XmtF2rtbw%253D&amp;md5=991acc34b1abb661081497cb350f3869</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1021/cr400020d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Carenco, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Portehault, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Boissìere, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mézailles, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sanchez, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanoscaled Metal Borides and Phosphides: Recent Developments and Perspectives</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanoscaled Metal Borides and Phosphides: Recent Developments and Perspectives</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Carenco, Sophie; Portehault, David; Boissiere, Cedric; Mezailles, Nicolas; Sanchez, Clement</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">113</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7981-8065</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHREAY</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The wide variety of nanostructured metal boride and metal phosphide nanoparticles that can be obtained nowadays, and the strategies that researchers successfully developed to reach these nanoscaled phases are summarized.  The development of nanoscaled metal borides is clearly less mature essentially for two reasons: the lack of highly reactive boron sources and the resistance to crystn.  While mechanistic studies could shed light on the crucial exptl. levers for better compositional, structural, and morphol. control of nanostructured metal phosphides and metal borides, another tantalizing approach is to rethink the existing syntheses in terms of precursors, in an attempt to rationalize the stoichiometry introduced and the reaction temp.  The use of single-source precursors provides an interesting soln. by bringing the constituents in the right stoichiometry and already intertwined at the mol. scale.  A third path is to use very reactive P or B sources to ensure a quant. incorporation in the final nanomaterials.  The two families of materials are not mirrored in this aspect since elemental phosphorus P4 is highly reactive while elemental boron allotropes are very stable.  Relying on specific reaction media that can enhance kinetics, like specific solvents (e.g., salt melts) for metal borides, is another tempting soln.  The properties of nanoscaled metal borides and metal phosphides are shown to differ, if not outperform in some cases, those of the traditional bulk phases because of increased surface exposure or grain boundary d., and also confinement, like quantum effects in semiconductors.  The behavior of these nanoscaled cryst. phases in electronics, optics, magnetism, mechanics, and biomedicine are still to be explored, esp. because most of these compds. have not been obtained yet at the nanoscale in the strict sense: one particle dimension below 100 nm.  Future works may then first conc. on a better rationalization of the synthetic procedures, in order to further develop applications, for instance in the field of catalysis.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoL1xza0rz5-LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh7OX_i7J51gg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXpsFWru7k%253D&amp;md5=78ea6f2247dea47f9744aeef861c8338</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1039/C5EE02074C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kopold, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Aken, P.
